solid #aaa"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Media_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Media of the United Kingdom">Media of the United Kingdom</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of newspapers in the United Kingdom">List of newspapers</a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>The Guardian</b></i> is a British daily newspaper.  It was known from 1821 until 1959 as the <i><b>Manchester Guardian</b></i>. Along with its sister papers <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Guardian_Weekly" class="mw-redirect" title="Guardian Weekly">Guardian Weekly</a></i>,  <i>The Guardian</i> is part of the <a href="/wiki/Guardian_Media_Group" title="Guardian Media Group">Guardian Media Group</a>, owned by the <a href="/wiki/Scott_Trust_Limited" title="Scott Trust Limited">Scott Trust</a>. The Trust was created in 1936 "to secure the financial and editorial independence of the <i>Guardian</i> in perpetuity and to safeguard the journalistic freedom and liberal values of the <i>Guardian</i> free from commercial or political interference." The <i>Scott Trust</i> was converted into a limited company in 2008, with a constitution written so as to project the same protections for <i>the Guardian</i> as were originally built into the very structure of the Trust by its creators. Profits are reinvested in journalism rather than to benefit an owner or shareholders.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The paper\'s readership is generally on the mainstream left of British political opinion.<sup id="cite_ref-International_Socialism_2003_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-International_Socialism_2003-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ipsos_MORI_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ipsos_MORI-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The newspaper\'s reputation as a platform for <a href="/wiki/Social_liberalism" title="Social liberalism">liberal</a> and left-wing editorial has led to the use of the "<i>Guardian</i> reader" and "Guardianista" as often (but not always) pejorative epithets for those of left-leaning or <a href="/wiki/Politically_correct" class="mw-redirect" title="Politically correct">politically correct</a> tendencies.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-What_the_papers_say_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_papers_say-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Guardian</i> is edited by <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Viner" title="Katharine Viner">Katharine Viner</a>, who succeeded <a href="/wiki/Alan_Rusbridger" title="Alan Rusbridger">Alan Rusbridger</a> in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian200315_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian200315-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RusbridgerFarewell_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RusbridgerFarewell-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, <i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> print edition had an average <a href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_the_United_Kingdom_by_circulation" title="List of newspapers in the United Kingdom by circulation">daily circulation</a> of roughly 162,000 copies in the country, behind <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-circ_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-circ-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Since 2018 it has been published in <a href="/wiki/Tabloid_(newspaper_format)" title="Tabloid (newspaper format)">tabloid format</a>.  The newspaper has <a href="/wiki/TheGuardian.com" title="TheGuardian.com">an online UK edition</a> as well as two international websites, <i><a href="/wiki/Guardian_Australia" title="Guardian Australia">Guardian Australia</a></i> (founded in 2013) and <i><a href="/wiki/Guardian_US" title="Guardian US">Guardian US</a></i> (founded in 2011). The newspaper\'s <a href="/wiki/Online_newspaper" title="Online newspaper">online edition</a> was the fifth most widely read in the world in October 2014, with over 42.6 million readers.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Its combined print and online editions reach nearly 9 million British readers.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Notable <a href="/wiki/Scoop_(news)" title="Scoop (news)">scoops</a> include the 2011 <a href="/wiki/News_International_phone_hacking_scandal" title="News International phone hacking scandal">News International phone hacking scandal</a>, in particular the hacking of murdered English teenager <a href="/wiki/Murder_of_Milly_Dowler" title="Murder of Milly Dowler">Milly Dowler</a>\'s phone.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The investigation led to the closure of the UK\'s biggest selling Sunday newspaper, and one of the highest circulation newspapers in the world, the <i><a href="/wiki/News_of_the_World" title="News of the World">News of the World</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The newspaper also released news of the secret collection of <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Communications" title="Verizon Communications">Verizon</a> telephone records held by US President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>\'s administration in June 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-reuters.com_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and subsequently revealed the existence of the <a href="/wiki/PRISM_(surveillance_program)" title="PRISM (surveillance program)">PRISM surveillance program</a> after it was leaked to the paper by <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">NSA</a> <a href="/wiki/Whistleblower" title="Whistleblower">whistleblower</a> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Snowden" title="Edward Snowden">Edward Snowden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-theguardian.com_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian.com-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, it led the investigation into the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Papers" title="Panama Papers">Panama Papers</a>, exposing the links of then British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> to <a href="/wiki/Offshore_bank" title="Offshore bank">offshore bank accounts</a>. <i>The Guardian</i> has been named Newspaper of the Year four times at the annual <a href="/wiki/British_Press_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="British Press Awards">British Press Awards</a>, the most recent in 2014 for reporting on government surveillance.<sup id="cite_ref-award_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-award-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Because of the frequency of the paper\'s <a href="/wiki/Typographical_error" title="Typographical error">typographical errors</a>, in the 1960s <i><a href="/wiki/Private_Eye" title="Private Eye">Private Eye</a></i> dubbed it <i>The Grauniad</i>, a nickname still used today.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div tocsection-17"><a href="#Guardian_America"><span class="tocnumber">1.3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Guardian America</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-18"><a href="#Gagged_from_reporting_Parliament"><span class="tocnumber">1.3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Gagged from reporting Parliament</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-19"><a href="#Edward_Snowden_leaks_and_intervention_by_the_UK_government"><span class="tocnumber">1.3.5</span> <span class="toctext">Edward Snowden leaks and intervention by the UK government</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Ownership_and_finances"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Ownership and finances</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#&quot;Membership&quot;_subscription_scheme"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">"Membership" subscription scheme</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Foundation_funding"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Foundation funding</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Political_stance_and_editorial_opinion"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Political stance and editorial opinion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Controversy"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Controversy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Circulation_and_format"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Circulation and format</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Publication_history"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Publication history</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Moving_to_the_Berliner_paper_format"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Moving to the Berliner paper format</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-28"><a href="#Reception"><span class="tocnumber">5.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Reception</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Tabloid_format_since_2018"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Tabloid format since 2018</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Regular_content_and_features"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Regular content and features</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Every_weekday_content"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Every weekday content</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Day-specific_content"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Day-specific content</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Online_media"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Online media</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#GuardianFilms"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">GuardianFilms</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#References_in_popular_culture"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References in popular culture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Awards"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Awards</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Received"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Received</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Given"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">Given</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Best_books_lists"><span class="tocnumber">10.3</span> <span class="toctext">Best books lists</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#Editors"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Editors</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#Notable_regular_contributors_(past_and_present)"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Notable regular contributors (past and present)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#Guardian_News_&amp;_Media_Archive"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Guardian News &amp; Media Archive</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#Notes_and_references"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Notes and references</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1821_to_1972">1821 to 1972</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1821 to 1972">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Manchester_Guardian,_May_5_1821.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/The_Manchester_Guardian%2C_May_5_1821.jpg/220px-The_Manchester_Guardian%2C_May_5_1821.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/The_Manchester_Guardian%2C_May_5_1821.jpg/330px-The_Manchester_Guardian%2C_May_5_1821.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/The_Manchester_Guardian%2C_May_5_1821.jpg/440px-The_Manchester_Guardian%2C_May_5_1821.jpg 2x" data-file-width="667" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Manchester_Guardian,_May_5_1821.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Manchester Guardian</i> Prospectus, 1821</div></div></div>\n<p><i>The Manchester Guardian</i> was founded in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> in 1821 by cotton merchant <a href="/wiki/John_Edward_Taylor" title="John Edward Taylor">John Edward Taylor</a> with backing from the <a href="/wiki/Little_Circle" title="Little Circle">Little Circle</a>, a group of <a href="/wiki/Nonconformism" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonconformism">non-conformist</a> businessmen.<sup id="cite_ref-G_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> They launched their paper after the police closure of the more <a href="/wiki/Radicalism_(historical)#Popular_agitation" title="Radicalism (historical)">radical</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Manchester_Observer" title="Manchester Observer">Manchester Observer</a></i>, a paper that had championed the cause of the <a href="/wiki/Peterloo_Massacre" title="Peterloo Massacre">Peterloo Massacre</a> protesters.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Taylor had been hostile to the radical reformers, writing: "They have appealed not to the reason but the passions and the suffering of their abused and credulous fellow-countrymen, from whose ill-requited industry they extort for themselves the means of a plentiful and comfortable existence. They do not toil, neither do they spin, but they live better than those that do."<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> When the government closed down the <i>Manchester Observer</i>, the mill-owners\' champions had the upper hand.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The influential journalist <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_Garnett" title="Jeremiah Garnett">Jeremiah Garnett</a> joined Taylor during the establishment of the paper, and all of the Little Circle wrote articles for the new paper.<sup id="cite_ref-dnbGarnett_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnbGarnett-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The prospectus announcing the new publication proclaimed that it would "zealously enforce the principles of civil and religious Liberty [\xe2\x80\xa6] warmly advocate the cause of Reform [\xe2\x80\xa6] endeavour to assist in the diffusion of just principles of Political Economy and [\xe2\x80\xa6] support, without reference to the party from which they emanate, all serviceable measures".<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In 1825 the paper merged with the <i>British Volunteer</i> and was known as <i>The Manchester Guardian and British Volunteer</i> until 1828.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The working-class <i>Manchester and Salford Advertiser</i> called the <i>Manchester Guardian</i> "the foul <a href="/wiki/Prostitution" title="Prostitution">prostitute</a> and dirty <a href="/wiki/Parasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Parasite">parasite</a> of the worst portion of the mill-owners".<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Manchester Guardian</i> was generally hostile to labour\'s claims. Of the 1832 Ten Hours Bill, the paper doubted whether in view of the foreign competition "the passing of a law positively enacting a gradual destruction of the cotton manufacture in this kingdom would be a much less rational procedure."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Manchester Guardian</i> dismissed strikes as the work of outside agitators: "[\xe2\x80\xa6]&#160;if an accommodation can be effected, the occupation of the agents of the Union is gone. They live on strife [\xe2\x80\xa6]"<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Manchester Guardian</i> was highly critical of US President <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>\'s conduct during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">US Civil War</a>, writing on the news that Abraham Lincoln had been assassinated: "Of his rule, we can never speak except as a series of acts abhorrent to every true notion of constitutional right and human liberty [\xe2\x80\xa6]"<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="C._P._Scott">C. P. Scott</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: C. P. Scott">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/C._P._Scott" title="C. P. Scott">C. P. Scott</a> made the newspaper nationally recognised. He was editor for 57 years from 1872, and became its owner when he bought the paper from the estate of Taylor\'s son in 1907. Under Scott, the paper\'s moderate editorial line became more radical, supporting <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Gladstone</a> when the Liberals split in 1886, and opposing the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a> against popular opinion.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Scott supported the movement for <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women&#39;s suffrage">women\'s suffrage</a>, but was critical of any tactics by the <a href="/wiki/Suffragettes" class="mw-redirect" title="Suffragettes">Suffragettes</a> that involved <a href="/wiki/Direct_action" title="Direct action">direct action</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-suffragettes_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suffragettes-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> "The really ludicrous position is that <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">Mr Lloyd George</a> is fighting to enfranchise seven million women and the militants are smashing unoffending people\'s windows and breaking up benevolent societies\' meetings in a desperate effort to prevent him." Scott thought the Suffragettes\' "courage and devotion" was "worthy of a better cause and saner leadership".<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> It has been argued that Scott\'s criticism reflected a widespread disdain, at the time, for those women who "transgressed the gender expectations of <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Edwardian Era">Edwardian society</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-suffragettes_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suffragettes-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scott commissioned <a href="/wiki/J._M._Synge" class="mw-redirect" title="J. M. Synge">J. M. Synge</a> and his friend <a href="/wiki/Jack_Yeats" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack Yeats">Jack Yeats</a> to produce articles and drawings documenting the social conditions of the west of Ireland (pre-First World War), and these pieces were published in 1911 in the collection <i>Travels in Wicklow, West Kerry and Connemara</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scott\'s friendship with <a href="/wiki/Chaim_Weizmann" title="Chaim Weizmann">Chaim Weizmann</a> played a role in the <a href="/wiki/Balfour_Declaration_of_1917" class="mw-redirect" title="Balfour Declaration of 1917">Balfour Declaration of 1917</a>, and in 1948 <i>The Manchester Guardian</i> was a supporter of the new State of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.\n</p><p>In June 1936 ownership of the paper passed to the <i><a href="/wiki/Scott_Trust" class="mw-redirect" title="Scott Trust">Scott Trust</a></i> (named after the last owner, John Russell Scott, who was the first chairman of the Trust). This move ensured the paper\'s independence.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_Civil_War">Spanish Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Spanish Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Traditionally affiliated with the centrist to centre-left <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a>, and with a northern, non-conformist circulation base, the paper earned a national reputation and the respect of the left during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> (1936\xe2\x80\x931939). <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a> writes in <i><a href="/wiki/Homage_to_Catalonia#Appendix_one" title="Homage to Catalonia">Homage to Catalonia</a></i>: "Of our larger papers, the <i>Manchester Guardian</i> is the only one that leaves me with an increased respect for its honesty". With the pro-Liberal <i><a href="/wiki/News_Chronicle" title="News Chronicle">News Chronicle</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a>-supporting <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Herald_(UK_newspaper)" title="Daily Herald (UK newspaper)">Daily Herald</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Great_Britain" title="Communist Party of Great Britain">Communist Party</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Morning_Star_(British_newspaper)" title="Morning Star (British newspaper)">Daily Worker</a></i> and several Sunday and weekly papers, it supported the Republican government against General <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a>\'s insurgent nationalists.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war">Post-war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Post-war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The paper\'s then editor, <a href="/wiki/A._P._Wadsworth" title="A. P. Wadsworth">A. P. Wadsworth</a>, so loathed Labour\'s <a href="/wiki/Left-wing" class="mw-redirect" title="Left-wing">left-wing</a> champion <a href="/wiki/Aneurin_Bevan" title="Aneurin Bevan">Aneurin Bevan</a>, who had made a reference to getting rid of "Tory Vermin" in a speech "and the hate-gospellers of his entourage" that it encouraged readers to vote Conservative and remove <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Attlee</a>\'s post-war Labour government.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The newspaper opposed the creation of the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> as it feared the state provision of healthcare would "eliminate selective elimination" and lead to an increase of congenitally deformed and feckless people.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Manchester Guardian</i> strongly opposed military intervention during the 1956 <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>: "The Anglo-French ultimatum to Egypt is an act of folly, without justification in any terms but brief expediency. It pours petrol on a growing fire. There is no knowing what kind of explosion will follow."<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 24 August 1959, <i>The Manchester Guardian</i> changed its name to <i>The Guardian</i>. This change reflected the growing prominence of national and international affairs in the newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In September 1961, <i>The Guardian</i>, which had previously only been published in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>, began to be printed in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1972_to_2000">1972 to 2000</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1972 to 2000">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Ireland_conflict">Northern Ireland conflict</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Northern Ireland conflict">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>When 13 civil rights demonstrators in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> were killed by British soldiers on 30 January 1972 (known as <a href="/wiki/Bloody_Sunday_(1972)" title="Bloody Sunday (1972)">Bloody Sunday</a>), <i>The Guardian</i> said that "Neither side can escape condemnation."<sup id="cite_ref-BloodySunday_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BloodySunday-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Of the protesters, they wrote, "The organizers of the demonstration, Miss <a href="/wiki/Bernadette_Devlin_McAliskey" title="Bernadette Devlin McAliskey">Bernadette Devlin</a> among them, deliberately challenged the ban on marches. They knew that stone throwing and <a href="/wiki/Sniping" class="mw-redirect" title="Sniping">sniping</a> could not be prevented, and that the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">IRA</a> might use the <a href="/wiki/Human_shield" title="Human shield">crowd as a shield</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-BloodySunday_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BloodySunday-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Of the army, they wrote, "there seems little doubt that random shots were fired into the crowd, that aim was taken at individuals who were neither bombers nor weapons carriers and that excessive force was used".<sup id="cite_ref-BloodySunday_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BloodySunday-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many Irish people believed that the <a href="/wiki/Widgery_Tribunal" class="mw-redirect" title="Widgery Tribunal">Widgery Tribunal</a>\'s ruling on the killings was a whitewash,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> a view that was later supported with the publication of the <a href="/wiki/Saville_inquiry" class="mw-redirect" title="Saville inquiry">Saville inquiry</a> in 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> but in 1972 <i>The Guardian</i> declared that "Widgery\'s report is not one-sided" (20 April 1972).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> At the time the paper also supported <a href="/wiki/Internment" title="Internment">internment</a> without trial in Northern Ireland: "Internment without trial is hateful, repressive and undemocratic. In the existing Irish situation, most regrettably, it is also inevitable... .To remove the ringleaders, in the hope that the atmosphere might calm down, is a step to which there is no obvious alternative."<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Before then, <i>The Guardian</i> had called for British troops to be sent to the region: British soldiers could "present a more disinterested face of law and order,"<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> but only on condition that "Britain takes charge."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sarah_Tisdall">Sarah Tisdall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Sarah Tisdall">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 1983 the paper was at the centre of a controversy surrounding documents regarding the stationing of <a href="/wiki/Cruise_missile" title="Cruise missile">cruise missiles</a> in Britain that were leaked to <i>The Guardian</i> by civil servant <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Tisdall" title="Sarah Tisdall">Sarah Tisdall</a>. The paper eventually complied with a court order to hand over the documents to the authorities, which resulted in a six-month prison sentence for Tisdall,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> though she served only four. "I still blame myself," said <a href="/wiki/Peter_Preston" title="Peter Preston">Peter Preston</a>, who was the editor of <i>The Guardian</i> at the time, but he went on to argue that the paper had no choice because it "believed in the rule of law".<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Gulf_War">First Gulf War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: First Gulf War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In the lead-up to the first <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, between 1990 and 1991, <i>The Guardian</i> expressed doubts about military action against Iraq: "Frustration in the Gulf leads temptingly to the invocation of task forces and tactical bombing, but the military option is no option at all. The emergence yesterday of a potential hostage problem of vast dimensions only emphasised that this is far too complex a crisis for gunboat diplomacy. Loose talk of \'carpet bombing\' Baghdad should be put back in the bottle of theoretical but unacceptable scenarios."<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:First_Gulf_War_Plaque,_Stafford_War_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1405400.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/First_Gulf_War_Plaque%2C_Stafford_War_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1405400.jpg/220px-First_Gulf_War_Plaque%2C_Stafford_War_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1405400.jpg" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/First_Gulf_War_Plaque%2C_Stafford_War_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1405400.jpg/330px-First_Gulf_War_Plaque%2C_Stafford_War_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1405400.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/First_Gulf_War_Plaque%2C_Stafford_War_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1405400.jpg/440px-First_Gulf_War_Plaque%2C_Stafford_War_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1405400.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="523" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:First_Gulf_War_Plaque,_Stafford_War_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1405400.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First Gulf War Plaque, Stafford War Memorial</div></div></div>\n<p>But on the eve of the war, the paper rallied to the war cause: "The simple cause, at the end, is just. An evil regime in Iraq instituted an evil and brutal invasion. Our soldiers and airmen are there, at UN behest, to set that evil to rights. Their duties are clear.&#160;... Let the momentum, and the resolution, be swift."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> After the event, journalist <a href="/wiki/Maggie_O%27Kane" title="Maggie O&#39;Kane">Maggie O\'Kane</a> conceded that she and her colleagues had been a mouthpiece for war propaganda: "...&#160;we, the media, were harnessed like 2,000 beach donkeys and led through the sand to see what the British and US military wanted us to see in this nice clean war".<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alleged_penetration_by_Russian_intelligence">Alleged penetration by Russian intelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Alleged penetration by Russian intelligence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 1994, <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a> defector <a href="/wiki/Oleg_Gordievsky" title="Oleg Gordievsky">Oleg Gordievsky</a> identified <i>Guardian</i> literary editor <a href="/wiki/Richard_Gott" title="Richard Gott">Richard Gott</a> as "an agent of influence". While Gott denied that he received cash, he admitted he had had lunch at the Soviet Embassy and had taken benefits from the KGB on overseas visits. Gott resigned from his post.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gordievsky commented on the newspaper: "The KGB loved <i>The Guardian</i>. It was deemed highly susceptible to penetration."<sup id="cite_ref-findarticles.com_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-findarticles.com-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jonathan_Aitken">Jonathan Aitken</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Jonathan Aitken">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 1995, both the <a href="/wiki/Granada_Television" class="mw-redirect" title="Granada Television">Granada Television</a> programme <i><a href="/wiki/World_In_Action" class="mw-redirect" title="World In Action">World In Action</a></i> and <i>The Guardian</i> were sued for <a href="/wiki/Libel" class="mw-redirect" title="Libel">libel</a> by the then cabinet minister <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Aitken#Libel,_arrest_and_prison" title="Jonathan Aitken">Jonathan Aitken</a>, for their allegation that <a href="/wiki/Harrods" title="Harrods">Harrods</a> owner <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Al_Fayed" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohamed Al Fayed">Mohamed Al Fayed</a> had paid for Aitken and his wife to stay at the <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B4tel_Ritz_Paris" title="H\xc3\xb4tel Ritz Paris">H\xc3\xb4tel Ritz</a> in Paris, which would have amounted to accepting a bribe on Aitken\'s part. Aitken publicly stated that he would fight with "the simple sword of truth and the trusty shield of British fair play".<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The court case proceeded, and in 1997 <i>The Guardian</i> produced evidence that Aitken\'s claim of his wife paying for the hotel stay was untrue.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In 1999, Aitken was jailed for <a href="/wiki/Perjury" title="Perjury">perjury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perverting_the_course_of_justice" title="Perverting the course of justice">perverting the course of justice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kosovo_War">Kosovo War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Kosovo War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The paper supported <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>\'s military intervention in the <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_War" title="Kosovo War">Kosovo War</a> in 1998\xe2\x80\x931999. <i>The Guardian</i> stated that "the only honourable course for Europe and America is to use military force".<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mary_Kaldor" title="Mary Kaldor">Mary Kaldor</a>\'s piece was headlined "Bombs away! But to save civilians, we must get in some soldiers too."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_2000">Since 2000</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Since 2000">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Guardian_senior_news_writer_Esther_Addley_interviewing_Ecuadorian_Foreign_Minister_Ricardo_Pati%C3%B1o.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/The_Guardian_senior_news_writer_Esther_Addley_interviewing_Ecuadorian_Foreign_Minister_Ricardo_Pati%C3%B1o.jpg/220px-The_Guardian_senior_news_writer_Esther_Addley_interviewing_Ecuadorian_Foreign_Minister_Ricardo_Pati%C3%B1o.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/The_Guardian_senior_news_writer_Esther_Addley_interviewing_Ecuadorian_Foreign_Minister_Ricardo_Pati%C3%B1o.jpg/330px-The_Guardian_senior_news_writer_Esther_Addley_interviewing_Ecuadorian_Foreign_Minister_Ricardo_Pati%C3%B1o.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/The_Guardian_senior_news_writer_Esther_Addley_interviewing_Ecuadorian_Foreign_Minister_Ricardo_Pati%C3%B1o.jpg/440px-The_Guardian_senior_news_writer_Esther_Addley_interviewing_Ecuadorian_Foreign_Minister_Ricardo_Pati%C3%B1o.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5616" data-file-height="3744" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Guardian_senior_news_writer_Esther_Addley_interviewing_Ecuadorian_Foreign_Minister_Ricardo_Pati%C3%B1o.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Guardian</i> senior news writer Esther Addley interviewing Ecuadorian foreign minister <a href="/wiki/Ricardo_Pati%C3%B1o" title="Ricardo Pati\xc3\xb1o">Ricardo Pati\xc3\xb1o</a> for an article relating to <a href="/wiki/Julian_Assange" title="Julian Assange">Julian Assange</a> in 2014.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the early 2000s, <i>The Guardian</i> challenged the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Settlement_1701" title="Act of Settlement 1701">Act of Settlement 1701</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Treason_Felony_Act_1848" title="Treason Felony Act 1848">Treason Felony Act 1848</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In October 2004, <i>The Guardian</i> published a humorous column by <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Brooker" title="Charlie Brooker">Charlie Brooker</a> in its entertainment guide, which appeared to call for the assassination of <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> This caused some controversy and the paper was forced to issue an apology and remove the article from its website.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/7_July_2005_London_bombings" title="7 July 2005 London bombings">7 July 2005 London bombings</a>, <i>The Guardian</i> published an article on its comment pages by <a href="/wiki/Dilpazier_Aslam" title="Dilpazier Aslam">Dilpazier Aslam</a>, a 27-year-old British Muslim and journalism trainee from <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Aslam was a member of <a href="/wiki/Hizb_ut-Tahrir" title="Hizb ut-Tahrir">Hizb ut-Tahrir</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Islamist" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamist">Islamist</a> group, and had published a number of articles on their website. According to the paper, it did not know that Aslam was a member of Hizb ut-Tahrir when he applied to become a trainee, though several staff members were informed of this once he started at the paper.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Home_Office" title="Home Office">Home Office</a> has claimed the group\'s "ultimate aim is the establishment of an Islamic state (Caliphate), according to Hizb ut-Tahrir via non-violent means". <i>The Guardian</i> asked Aslam to resign his membership of the group and, when he did not do so, terminated his employment.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In early 2009, the paper started a tax investigation into a number of major UK companies,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> including publishing a database of the tax paid by the <a href="/wiki/FTSE_100" class="mw-redirect" title="FTSE 100">FTSE 100</a> companies.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Internal documents relating to <a href="/wiki/Barclays_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Barclays Bank">Barclays Bank</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Tax_avoidance" title="Tax avoidance">tax avoidance</a> were removed from <i>The Guardian</i> website after Barclays obtained a <a href="/wiki/Gagging_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Gagging order">gagging order</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The paper played a pivotal role in exposing the depth of the <a href="/wiki/News_of_the_World_phone_hacking_affair" class="mw-redirect" title="News of the World phone hacking affair"><i>News of the World</i> phone hacking affair</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> <i><a href="/wiki/Intelligent_Life_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Intelligent Life (magazine)">Intelligent Life</a></i> magazine opined that... \n</p>\n<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">\xe2\x80\x9c\n</td>\n<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">As <a href="/wiki/Watergate_scandal" title="Watergate scandal">Watergate</a> is to the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">Washington Post</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Thalidomide" title="Thalidomide">thalidomide</a> to the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Times" title="The Sunday Times">Sunday Times</a></i>, so <a href="/wiki/News_International_phone_hacking_scandal" title="News International phone hacking scandal">phone-hacking</a> will surely be to the <i>Guardian</i>: a defining moment in its history.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">\xe2\x80\x9d\n</td></tr>\n\n</tbody></table>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Accusations_of_anti-Semitism_and_bias_in_coverage_of_Israel">Accusations of anti-Semitism and bias in coverage of Israel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Accusations of anti-Semitism and bias in coverage of Israel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In recent decades <i>The Guardian</i> has been accused of biased <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_the_Israeli_government" title="Criticism of the Israeli government">criticism of Israeli government policy</a><sup id="cite_ref-Sela_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sela-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and of bias against the Palestinians.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In December 2003, columnist <a href="/wiki/Julie_Burchill" title="Julie Burchill">Julie Burchill</a> cited "striking bias against the state of Israel" as one of the reasons she left the paper for <i>The Times</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> A leaked report from the European Monitoring Centre on Racism cited <i>The Economist</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> claim that for "many British Jews," the British media\'s reporting on Israel "is spiced with a tone of animosity, \'as to smell of <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">anti-Semitism</a>\'&#160;... This is above all the case with the <i>Guardian</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i>". The EU said the report, dated February 2003, was not published because it was insubstantial in its current state and lacking sufficient evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Responding to these accusations, a <i>Guardian</i> editorial in 2002 condemned <a href="/wiki/Anti-Semitism" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Semitism">anti-Semitism</a> and defended the paper\'s right to criticise the policies and actions of the Israeli government, arguing that those who view such criticism as inherently anti-Jewish are mistaken.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Harriet Sherwood, then <i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> foreign editor, later its Jerusalem correspondent, has also denied that <i>The Guardian</i> has an anti-Israel bias, saying that the paper aims to cover all viewpoints in the <a href="/wiki/Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict" title="Israeli\xe2\x80\x93Palestinian conflict">Israeli\xe2\x80\x93Palestinian conflict</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 6 November 2011, Chris Elliott, the <i>Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s readers\' editor, wrote that "<i>Guardian</i> reporters, writers and editors must be more vigilant about the language they use when writing about Jews or Israel," citing recent cases where <i>The Guardian</i> received complaints regarding language chosen to describe Jews or Israel. Elliott noted that, over nine months, he upheld complaints regarding language in certain articles that were seen as anti-Semitic, revising the language and footnoting this change.<sup id="cite_ref-Elliott_responds_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliott_responds-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s style guide section referred to <a href="/wiki/Tel_Aviv" title="Tel Aviv">Tel Aviv</a> as the capital of Israel in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Guardian</i> later clarified: "In 1980, the Israeli Knesset enacted a law designating the city of Jerusalem, including East Jerusalem, as the country\'s capital. In response, the UN security council issued resolution 478, censuring the "change in character and status of the Holy City of Jerusalem" and calling on all member states with diplomatic missions in the city to withdraw. The UN has reaffirmed this position on several occasions, and almost every country now has its embassy in Tel Aviv. While it was therefore right to issue a correction to make clear Israel\'s designation of Jerusalem as its capital is not recognised by the international community, we accept that it is wrong to state that Tel Aviv \xe2\x80\x93 the country\'s financial and diplomatic centre \xe2\x80\x93 is the capital. The style guide has been amended accordingly."<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_retract_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_retract-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 11 August 2014 the print edition of <i>The Guardian</i> published a pro-Israeli advocacy advert during the <a href="/wiki/2014_Israel%E2%80%93Gaza_conflict" title="2014 Israel\xe2\x80\x93Gaza conflict">2014 Israel\xe2\x80\x93Gaza conflict</a> featuring <a href="/wiki/Elie_Wiesel" title="Elie Wiesel">Elie Wiesel</a>, headed by the words "Jews rejected child sacrifice 3,500 years ago. Now it\'s Hamas\' turn." <i>The Times</i> had decided against running the ad, although it had already appeared in major American newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> One week later, Chris Elliott expressed the opinion that the newspaper should have rejected the language used in the advert and should have negotiated with the advertiser on this matter.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Clark_County">Clark County</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Clark County">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In August 2004, for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2004" title="United States presidential election, 2004">US presidential election</a>, the daily <i>G2</i> supplement launched an experimental letter-writing campaign in <a href="/wiki/Clark_County,_Ohio" title="Clark County, Ohio">Clark County, Ohio</a>, an average-sized county in a <a href="/wiki/Swing_state" title="Swing state">swing state</a>. The editor of the <i>G2</i> supplement <a href="/wiki/Ian_Katz" title="Ian Katz">Ian Katz</a> bought a voter list from the county for $25 and asked readers to write to people listed as undecided in the election, giving them an impression of the international view and the importance of voting against President George W. Bush.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The paper scrapped "Operation Clark County" on 21 October 2004 after first publishing a column of responses\xe2\x80\x94nearly all of them outraged\xe2\x80\x94to the campaign under the headline "Dear Limey assholes."<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Some commentators suggested that the public\'s dislike of the campaign contributed to Bush\'s victory in Clark County.<sup id="cite_ref-bowers_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowers-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Guardian_America">Guardian America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Guardian America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 2007, the paper launched <i>Guardian America</i>, an attempt to capitalise on its large online readership in the United States, which at the time stood at more than 5.9 million. The company hired former <i><a href="/wiki/American_Prospect" class="mw-redirect" title="American Prospect">American Prospect</a></i> editor, <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_(magazine)" title="New York (magazine)">New York</a></i> magazine columnist and <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Review_of_Books" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Review of Books">New York Review of Books</a></i> writer <a href="/wiki/Michael_Tomasky" title="Michael Tomasky">Michael Tomasky</a> to head the project and hire a staff of American reporters and web editors. The site featured news from <i>The Guardian</i> that was relevant to an American audience: coverage of US news and the Middle East, for example.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tomasky stepped down from his position as editor of <i>Guardian America</i> in February 2009, ceding editing and planning duties to other US and London staff. He retained his position as a columnist and blogger, taking the title editor-at-large.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2009, the company abandoned the <i>Guardian America</i> homepage, instead directing users to a US news index page on the main <i>Guardian</i> website.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The following month, the company laid off six American employees, including a reporter, a multimedia producer and four web editors. The move came as <i>Guardian News and Media</i> opted to reconsider its US strategy amid a huge effort to cut costs across the company.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In subsequent years, however, <i>The Guardian</i> has hired various commentators on US affairs including <a href="/wiki/Ana_Marie_Cox" title="Ana Marie Cox">Ana Marie Cox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Wolff_(journalist)" title="Michael Wolff (journalist)">Michael Wolff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naomi_Wolf" title="Naomi Wolf">Naomi Wolf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Greenwald" title="Glenn Greenwald">Glenn Greenwald</a> and George W. Bush\'s former speechwriter <a href="/wiki/Josh_Trevi%C3%B1o" class="mw-redirect" title="Josh Trevi\xc3\xb1o">Josh Trevi\xc3\xb1o</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Trevi\xc3\xb1o_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trevi\xc3\xb1o-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Trevi\xc3\xb1o\'s first blog post was an apology for a controversial tweet posted in June 2011 over the second Gaza flotilla, the controversy which had been revived by the appointment.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Guardian_US" title="Guardian US">Guardian US</a></i> launched in September 2011, led by editor-in-chief <a href="/wiki/Janine_Gibson" title="Janine Gibson">Janine Gibson</a>, which replaced the previous <i>Guardian America</i> service.<sup id="cite_ref-PressRel_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PressRel-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> After a period during which <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Viner" title="Katharine Viner">Katharine Viner</a> served as the US editor-in-chief before taking charge of <i>Guardian News and Media</i> as a whole, Viner\'s former deputy, Lee Glendinning, was appointed to succeed her as head of the American operation at the beginning of June 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gagged_from_reporting_Parliament">Gagged from reporting Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Gagged from reporting Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In October 2009, <i>The Guardian</i> reported that it was forbidden to report on a parliamentary matter \xe2\x80\x93 a question recorded in a Commons order paper, to be answered by a minister later that week.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The paper noted that it was being "forbidden from telling its readers why the paper is prevented\xe2\x80\x94for the first time in memory\xe2\x80\x94from reporting parliament. Legal obstacles, which cannot be identified, involve proceedings, which cannot be mentioned, on behalf of a client who must remain secret. The only fact the <i>Guardian</i> can report is that the case involves the London solicitors <a href="/wiki/Carter-Ruck" title="Carter-Ruck">Carter-Ruck</a>." The paper further claimed that this case appears "to call into question privileges guaranteeing free speech established under the <a href="/wiki/1688_Bill_of_Rights" class="mw-redirect" title="1688 Bill of Rights">1688 Bill of Rights</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The only parliamentary question mentioning Carter-Ruck in the relevant period was by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Farrelly" title="Paul Farrelly">Paul Farrelly</a> MP, in reference to legal action by <a href="/wiki/Barclays" title="Barclays">Barclays</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trafigura" title="Trafigura">Trafigura</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The part of the question referencing Carter-Ruck relates to the latter company\'s September 2009 gagging order on the publication of a 2006 internal report<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> into the <a href="/wiki/2006_C%C3%B4te_d%27Ivoire_toxic_waste_dump" class="mw-redirect" title="2006 C\xc3\xb4te d&#39;Ivoire toxic waste dump">2006 C\xc3\xb4te d\'Ivoire toxic waste dump</a> scandal, which involved a <a href="/wiki/Class_action" title="Class action">class action</a> case that the company only settled in September 2009 after <i>The Guardian</i> published some of the commodity trader\'s internal emails.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The reporting injunction was lifted the next day, as Carter-Ruck withdrew it before <i>The Guardian</i> could challenge it in the High Court.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alan_Rusbridger" title="Alan Rusbridger">Alan Rusbridger</a> credited the rapid back-down of Carter-Ruck to Twitter,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> as did a BBC article.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Edward_Snowden_leaks_and_intervention_by_the_UK_government">Edward Snowden leaks and intervention by the UK government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Edward Snowden leaks and intervention by the UK government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In June 2013, the newspaper broke news of the secret collection of <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Communications" title="Verizon Communications">Verizon</a> telephone records held by <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>\'s administration<sup id="cite_ref-reuters.com_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and subsequently revealed the existence of the <a href="/wiki/PRISM_(surveillance_program)" title="PRISM (surveillance program)">PRISM surveillance program</a> after it was leaked to the paper by former <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">NSA</a> contractor <a href="/wiki/Edward_Snowden" title="Edward Snowden">Edward Snowden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-theguardian.com_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian.com-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The newspaper was subsequently contacted by the British government\'s Cabinet Secretary, <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Heywood" title="Jeremy Heywood">Sir Jeremy Heywood</a>, under instruction from Prime Minister David Cameron and Deputy Prime Minister Nick Clegg, who ordered that the hard drives containing the information be destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> offices were then visited in July by agents from the UK\'s <a href="/wiki/Government_Communications_Headquarters" title="Government Communications Headquarters">GCHQ</a>, who supervised the destruction of the hard drives containing information acquired from Snowden.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In June 2014 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Register" title="The Register">The Register</a></i> reported that the information the government sought to suppress by destroying the hard drives related to the location of a "beyond top secret" internet monitoring base in <a href="/wiki/Seeb" title="Seeb">Seeb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>, and the close involvement of <a href="/wiki/BT_Group" title="BT Group">BT</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cable_%26_Wireless_plc" title="Cable &amp; Wireless plc">Cable &amp; Wireless</a> in intercepting internet communications.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julian_Assange" title="Julian Assange">Julian Assange</a> criticised the newspaper for not publishing the entirety of the content when it had the chance.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ownership_and_finances">Ownership and finances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Ownership and finances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><i>The Guardian</i> is part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Guardian_Media_Group" title="Guardian Media Group">Guardian Media Group</a></i> (GMG) of newspapers, radio stations and print media including; <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> Sunday newspaper, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian_Weekly" title="The Guardian Weekly">The Guardian Weekly</a></i> international newspaper, and new media\xe2\x80\x94<i>Guardian Abroad</i> website, and <i><a href="/wiki/Theguardian.com" class="mw-redirect" title="Theguardian.com">guardian.co.uk</a></i>. All the aforementioned were owned by <i><a href="/wiki/Scott_Trust_Limited" title="Scott Trust Limited">The Scott Trust</a></i>, a charitable foundation existing between 1936 and 2008, which aimed to ensure the paper\'s <a href="/wiki/Editorial_independence" title="Editorial independence">editorial independence</a> in perpetuity, maintaining its financial health to ensure it did not become vulnerable to take overs by for-profit media groups. At the beginning of October 2008, the <i>Scott Trust</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s assets were transferred to a new limited company, <i>The Scott Trust Limited</i>, with the intention being that the original trust would be wound up.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liz_Forgan" title="Liz Forgan">Dame Liz Forgan</a>, chair of the <i>Scott Trust</i>, reassured staff that the purposes of the new company remained the same as under the previous arrangements.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG/220px-The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG/330px-The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG/440px-The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3170" data-file-height="1940" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> headquarters in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p><i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> ownership by the <i>Scott Trust</i> is probably a factor in its being the only British national daily to conduct (since 2003) an annual social, ethical and environmental <a href="/wiki/Audit" title="Audit">audit</a> in which it examines, under the scrutiny of an independent external auditor, its own behaviour as a company.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> It is also the only British national daily newspaper to employ an internal ombudsman (called the "readers\' editor") to handle complaints and corrections.\n</p><p><i>The Guardian</i> and its parent groups participate in <a href="/wiki/Project_Syndicate" title="Project Syndicate">Project Syndicate</a>, established by <a href="/wiki/George_Soros" title="George Soros">George Soros</a>, and intervened in 1995 to save the <i><a href="/wiki/Mail_%26_Guardian" title="Mail &amp; Guardian">Mail &amp; Guardian</a></i> in South Africa. However, Guardian Media Group sold the majority of its shares of the <i>Mail &amp; Guardian</i> in 2002.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p><i>The Guardian</i> has been consistently loss-making. The National Newspaper division of GMG, which also includes <i>The Observer</i>, reported operating losses of \xc2\xa349.9m in 2006, up from \xc2\xa318.6m in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The paper was therefore heavily dependent on cross-subsidisation from profitable companies within the group.\n</p><p>The continual losses made by the National Newspaper division of the <i>Guardian Media Group</i> caused it to dispose of its Regional Media division by selling titles to competitor <i><a href="/wiki/Trinity_Mirror" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinity Mirror">Trinity Mirror</a></i> in March 2010. This included the flagship <i><a href="/wiki/Manchester_Evening_News" title="Manchester Evening News">Manchester Evening News</a></i>, and severed the historic link between that paper and <i>The Guardian</i>. The sale was in order to safeguard the future of <i>The Guardian</i> newspaper as is the intended purpose of the <i>Scott Trust</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-M.E.N_sold_to_Trinity_Mirror_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M.E.N_sold_to_Trinity_Mirror-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 2011 <i>Guardian News and Media</i> revealed increased annual losses of \xc2\xa333m and announced that it was looking to focus on its online edition for news coverage, leaving the print edition to contain more comments and features. It was also speculated that <i>The Guardian</i> might become the first British national daily paper to be fully online.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For the three years up to June 2012, the paper lost \xc2\xa3100,000 a day, which prompted <i>Intelligent Life</i> to question whether <i>The Guardian</i> could survive.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Between 2007 and 2014 <i>The Guardian Media Group</i> sold all their side businesses, of regional papers and online portals for classifieds and consolidated, into <i>The Guardian</i> as sole product. The sales let them acquire a capital stock of \xc2\xa3838.3m as of July 2014, supposed to guarantee the independence of <i>The Guardian</i> in perpetuity. In the first year, the paper made more losses than predicted, and in January 2016 the publishers announced, that <i>The Guardian</i> will cut 20 per cent of staff and costs within the next three years.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>  The newspaper is rare in calling for direct contributions "to deliver the independent journalism the world needs." <sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id=".22Membership.22_subscription_scheme"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Membership&quot;_subscription_scheme">"Membership" subscription scheme</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: &quot;Membership&quot; subscription scheme">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 2014, <i>The Guardian</i> launched a membership scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The scheme aims to reduce the financial losses incurred by <i>The Guardian</i> without introducing a <a href="/wiki/Paywall" title="Paywall">paywall</a>, thus maintaining open access to the website. Website readers can pay a monthly subscription, with three tiers available.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation_funding">Foundation funding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Foundation funding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 2016, the company established a U.S.-based philanthropic arm to raise money from individuals and organizations including think tanks and corporate foundations. The grants are focused by the donors on particular issues. By the following year, the organization had raised $1 million from the likes of <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Omidyar" title="Pierre Omidyar">Pierre Omidyar</a>\xe2\x80\x99s Humanity United, the <a href="/wiki/Skoll_Foundation" title="Skoll Foundation">Skoll Foundation</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Conrad_N._Hilton_Foundation" title="Conrad N. Hilton Foundation">Conrad N. Hilton Foundation</a> to finance reporting on topics including modern-day slavery and climate change. <i>The Guardian</i> has stated that it has secured $6 million "in multi-year funding commitments" thus far.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The new project developed from funding relationships which the paper already had with the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Foundation" title="Ford Foundation">Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Foundation" title="Rockefeller Foundation">Rockefeller</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bill_%26_Melinda_Gates_Foundation" title="Bill &amp; Melinda Gates Foundation">Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Gates had given the organization $5 million<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> for its Global Development webpage.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_stance_and_editorial_opinion">Political stance and editorial opinion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Guardian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Political stance and editorial opinion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Founded by textile traders and merchants, in its early years <i>The Guardian</i> had a reputation as "an organ of the middle class",<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> or in the words of C. P. Scott\'s son Ted, "a paper that will remain bourgeois to the last".<sup id="cite_ref-Hunter2003_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hunter2003-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Associated at first with the <a href="/wiki/Little_Circle" title="Little Circle">Little Circle</a> and hence with <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical liberalism</a> as expressed by the <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whigs</a> and later by the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a>, its political orientation underwent a decisive change after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, leading to a gradual alignment with <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Political_left" class="mw-redirect" title="Political left">political left</a> in general.\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Scott_Trust_Limited" title="Scott Trust Limited">Scott Trust</a></i> describes one of its "core purposes" to be "to secure the financial and editorial independence of the <i>Guardian</i> in perpetuity: as a quality national newspaper without party affiliation; remaining faithful to its liberal tradition".<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Affiliation_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Affiliation-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The paper\'s readership is generally on the mainstream left of British political opinion: a <a href="/wiki/MORI" class="mw-redirect" title="MORI">MORI</a> poll taken between April and June 2000 showed that 80 per cent of <i>Guardian</i> readers were Labour Party voters;<sup id="cite_ref-International_Socialism_2003_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-International_Socialism_2003-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> according to another MORI poll taken in 2005, 48 per cent of <i>Guardian</i> readers were Labour voters and 34 per cent <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrat</a> voters.<sup id="cite_ref-Ipsos_MORI_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ipsos_MORI-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The newspaper\'s reputation as a platform for <a href="/wiki/Social_liberalism" title="Social liberalism">liberal</a> and left-wing opinions has led to the use of the epithets "<i>Guardian</i> reader" and "Guardianista" for people holding such views, or as a stereotype of such people as middle class, earnest and <a href="/wiki/Politically_correct" class="mw-redirect" title="Politically correct">politically correct</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-What_the_papers_say_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_papers_say-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although the paper is often considered to be "linked inextricably" to the Labour Party,<sup id="cite_ref-Affiliation_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Affiliation-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> three of <i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> four leader writers joined the more centrist <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_(UK)" title="Social Democratic Party (UK)">Social Democratic Party</a> on its foundation in 1981. The paper was enthusiastic in its support for <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> in his successful bid to lead the Labour Party,<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> and to be elected Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> On 19 January 2003, two months before the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">2003 invasion of Iraq</a>, an Observer Editorial said: "Military intervention in the Middle East holds many dangers. But if we want a lasting peace it may be the only option.
